        <title>Genome sequencing provides insights into the evolution and antioxidant activity of Chinese bayberry | BMC Genomics | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. &amp; Zucc.) is an economically important fruit tree characterized by its juicy fruits rich in antioxidant compounds. Elucidating the genetic basis of the biosynthesis of active antioxidant compounds in bayberry is fundamental for genetic improvement of bayberry and industrial applications of the fruit&#8217;s antioxidant components. Here, we report the genome sequence of a multiple disease-resistant bayberry variety, &#8216;Zaojia&#8217;, in China, and the transcriptome dynamics in the course of fruit development. A 289.92 Mb draft genome was assembled, and 26,325 protein-encoding genes were predicted. Most of the M. rubra genes in the antioxidant signaling pathways had multiple copies, likely originating from tandem duplication events. Further, many of the genes found here present structural variations or amino acid changes in the conserved functional residues across species. The expression levels of antioxidant genes were generally higher in the early stages of fruit development, and were correlated with the higher levels of total flavonoids and antioxidant capacity, in comparison with the mature fruit stages. Based on both gene expression and biochemical analyses, five genes, namely, caffeoyl-CoA O-methyltransferase, anthocyanidin 3-O-glucosyltransferase, (+)-neomenthol dehydrogenase, gibberellin 2-oxidase, and squalene monooxygenase, were suggested to regulate the flavonoid, anthocyanin, monoterpenoid, diterpenoid, and sesquiterpenoid/triterpenoid levels, respectively, during fruit development. This study describes both the complete genome and transcriptome of M. rubra. The results provide an important basis for future research on the genetic improvement of M. rubra and contribute to the understanding of its genetic evolution. The genome sequences corresponding to representative antioxidant signaling pathways can help revealing useful traits and functional genes."/>

            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );

    <link rel="canonical" href="//bmcgenomics.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7"/>
    

        
        
        
        <meta property="og:url" content="https://bmcgenomics.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="BMC Genomics"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Genome sequencing provides insights into the evolution and antioxidant activity of Chinese bayberry"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. &amp;amp; Zucc.) is an economically important fruit tree characterized by its juicy fruits rich in antioxidant compounds. Elucidating the genetic basis of the biosynthesis of active antioxidant compounds in bayberry is fundamental for genetic improvement of bayberry and industrial applications of the fruit’s antioxidant components. Here, we report the genome sequence of a multiple disease-resistant bayberry variety, ‘Zaojia’, in China, and the transcriptome dynamics in the course of fruit development. A 289.92 Mb draft genome was assembled, and 26,325 protein-encoding genes were predicted. Most of the M. rubra genes in the antioxidant signaling pathways had multiple copies, likely originating from tandem duplication events. Further, many of the genes found here present structural variations or amino acid changes in the conserved functional residues across species. The expression levels of antioxidant genes were generally higher in the early stages of fruit development, and were correlated with the higher levels of total flavonoids and antioxidant capacity, in comparison with the mature fruit stages. Based on both gene expression and biochemical analyses, five genes, namely, caffeoyl-CoA O-methyltransferase, anthocyanidin 3-O-glucosyltransferase, (+)-neomenthol dehydrogenase, gibberellin 2-oxidase, and squalene monooxygenase, were suggested to regulate the flavonoid, anthocyanin, monoterpenoid, diterpenoid, and sesquiterpenoid/triterpenoid levels, respectively, during fruit development. This study describes both the complete genome and transcriptome of M. rubra. The results provide an important basis for future research on the genetic improvement of M. rubra and contribute to the understanding of its genetic evolution. The genome sequences corresponding to representative antioxidant signaling pathways can help revealing useful traits and functional genes."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12864.jpg"/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Chinese bayberry (<i>Myrica rubra</i> Sieb. &amp; Zucc.) is an economically important fruit tree characterized by its juicy fruits rich in antioxidant compounds. Elucidating the genetic basis of the biosynthesis of active antioxidant compounds in bayberry is fundamental for genetic improvement of bayberry and industrial applications of the fruit’s antioxidant components. Here, we report the genome sequence of a multiple disease-resistant bayberry variety, ‘Zaojia’, in China, and the transcriptome dynamics in the course of fruit development.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>A 289.92 Mb draft genome was assembled, and 26,325 protein-encoding genes were predicted. Most of the <i>M. rubra</i> genes in the antioxidant signaling pathways had multiple copies, likely originating from tandem duplication events. Further, many of the genes found here present structural variations or amino acid changes in the conserved functional residues across species. The expression levels of antioxidant genes were generally higher in the early stages of fruit development, and were correlated with the higher levels of total flavonoids and antioxidant capacity, in comparison with the mature fruit stages. Based on both gene expression and biochemical analyses, five genes, namely, caffeoyl-CoA O-methyltransferase, anthocyanidin 3-O-glucosyltransferase, (+)-neomenthol dehydrogenase, gibberellin 2-oxidase, and squalene monooxygenase, were suggested to regulate the flavonoid, anthocyanin, monoterpenoid, diterpenoid, and sesquiterpenoid/triterpenoid levels, respectively, during fruit development.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>This study describes both the complete genome and transcriptome of <i>M. rubra</i>. The results provide an important basis for future research on the genetic improvement of <i>M. rubra</i> and contribute to the understanding of its genetic evolution. The genome sequences corresponding to representative antioxidant signaling pathways can help revealing useful traits and functional genes.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The Myricaceae family comprises three genera, of which <i>Myrica</i>, comprising approximately 50 species, is most widely distributed across the warm, humid regions of Asia (e.g., China, Japan, and India), Europe (e.g., France, Norway, Switzerland, and Spain), Africa (e.g., Kenya), North America (e.g., USA), and South America (e.g., Brazil) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sun C, Huang H, Xu C, Li X, Chen K. Biological activities of extracts from Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. et Zucc.): a review. Plant Food Hum Nutr. 2013;68:97–106." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d6478e580">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mathiesen L, Malterud KE, Sund RB. Antioxidant activity of fruit exudate and C-methylated dihydrochalcones from Myrica gale. Planta Med. 1995;61(6):515–8." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d6478e580_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Erickson DL, Hamrick JL. Genetic and clonal diversity for Myrica cerifera along a spatiotemporal island chronosequence. Heredity (Edinb). 2003;90(1):25–32." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d6478e580_2">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Njung'e K, Muriuki G, Mwangi JW, Kuria KA. Analgesic and antipyretic effects of Myrica salicifolia (Myricaceae). Phytother Res. 2002;16(Suppl 1):S73–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d6478e583">4</a>]. Chinese bayberry (<i>Myrica rubra</i> Sieb. &amp; Zucc.) is an economically important fruit tree, and its cultivation area in China is about 334,000 ha with an annual yield of 950,000 t, approximately. In addition to producing flavorful fruit, different organs of Chinese bayberry are used in traditional Chinese medicine. Chinese bayberry extracts contain antioxidants against inflammation, allergies, diabetes, cancer, bacterial infections, and diarrhea, among other health issues [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Sun C, Huang H, Xu C, Li X, Chen K. Biological activities of extracts from Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. et Zucc.): a review. Plant Food Hum Nutr. 2013;68:97–106." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d6478e589">1</a>].</p><p>Extracts of <i>Myrica</i> spp. contain many different kinds of flavonoids, such as myricetin, anthocyanins and proanthocyanidins, cyanidin-3-glucoside, myricanol, and myricanone [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yan LJ, Yang HT, Duan HY, Wu JT, Qian P, Fan XW, et al. Myricitrin inhibits vascular adhesion molecule expression in TNF-α-stimulated vascular smooth muscle cells. Mol Med Rep. 2017;16(5):6354–9." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d6478e598">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fu Y, Qiao L, Cao Y, Zhou X, Liu Y, Ye X. Structural elucidation and antioxidant activities of proanthocyanidins from Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. et Zucc.) leaves. PLoS One. 2014;9(5):e96162." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6478e598_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang Y, Zhang XN, Xie WH, Zheng YX, Cao JP, Cao PR, et al. The growth of SGC-7901 tumor xenografts was suppressed by Chinese bayberry anthocyanin extract through upregulating KLF6 gene expression. Nutrients. 2016;8:599." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d6478e598_2">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen P, Lin X, Yang CH, Tang X, Chang YW, Zheng W, et al. Study on chemical profile and neuroprotective activity of Myrica rubra leaf extract. Molecules. 2017;22(7):1226." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6478e598_3">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Paul A, Das J, Das S, Samadder A, Khuda-Bukhsh AR. Anticancer potential of myricanone, a major bioactive component of Myrica cerifera: novel signaling cascade for accomplishing apoptosis. J Acupunct Meridian Stud. 2013a;6(4):188–98." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d6478e601">9</a>], and phenolic compounds, such as gallic acid, catechin, hydroxybenzoic acid, and coumaric acid. Myricanone induces apoptosis in HepG2 liver cancer cells through the generation of reactive oxygen species, depolarization of the mitochondrial membrane, early release of cytochrome-c, downregulation of heat shock protein 70, and activation of a caspase cascade [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paul A, Das S, Das J, Samadder A, Bishayee K, Sadhukhan R, et al. Diarylheptanoid-myricanone isolated from ethanolic extract of Myrica cerifera shows anticancer effects on HeLa and PC3 cell lines: signalling pathway and drug-DNA interaction. J Integr Med. 2013b;11(6):405–15." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6478e604">10</a>]. It also has anticancer effects on the HeLa (cervical cancer) and PC3 (prostate cancer) cell lines as it triggers caspase activation and suppresses cell proliferation by downregulating NF-κB and signal transducer and activators of transcription 3 signaling cascades [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Paul A, Das S, Das J, Samadder A, Bishayee K, Sadhukhan R, et al. Diarylheptanoid-myricanone isolated from ethanolic extract of Myrica cerifera shows anticancer effects on HeLa and PC3 cell lines: signalling pathway and drug-DNA interaction. J Integr Med. 2013b;11(6):405–15." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6478e607">10</a>]. Myricetin exerts selective cytotoxic, pro-apoptotic, and anti-metastatic effects on prostate cancer cells by inhibiting <i>PIM1</i> and disrupting the PIM1/CXCR4 interactions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Ye C, Zhang C, Huang H, Yang B, Xiao G, Kong D, et al. The natural compound myricetin effectively represses the malignant progression of prostate cancer by inhibiting PIM1 and disrupting the PIM1/CXCR4 interaction. Cell Physiol Biochem. 2018;48(3):1230–44." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d6478e614">11</a>]. Chinese bayberry leaf flavonoids and proanthocyanidins inhibit the growth of the ovarian cancer cell line A2780/CP70. The extract was found to increase the expression of cleaved caspases 3 and -7 and to induce apoptosis via an extracellular signal-regulated kinase-dependent caspase-9 activation intrinsic apoptotic pathway by upregulating the pro-apoptotic proteins (BAD and BAX) and downregulating the anti-apoptotic proteins (BCL-XL and BCL-2) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zhang Y, Chen S, Wei C, Rankin GO, Ye X, Chen YC. Flavonoids from Chinese bayberry leaves induced apoptosis and G1 cell cycle arrest via Erk pathway in ovarian cancer cells. Eur J Med Chem. 2018b;147:218–26." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6478e617">12</a>]. It also inhibits angiogenesis and induces G1 cell cycle arrest, further reducing the levels of reactive oxygen species and targeting the AKT/mTOR/P70S6K/4E-BP-1 pathway to reduce the expression of hypoxia-inducible factor 1-alpha and vascular endothelial growth factor, thus inhibiting angiogenesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zhang Y, Chen S, Wei C, Rankin GO, Ye X, Chen YC. Flavonoids from Chinese bayberry leaves induced apoptosis and G1 cell cycle arrest via Erk pathway in ovarian cancer cells. Eur J Med Chem. 2018b;147:218–26." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6478e620">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Zhang Y, Chen S, Wei C, Rankin GO, Ye X, Chen YC. Dietary compound proanthocyanidins from Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. et Zucc.) leaves attenuate chemotherapy-resistant ovarian cancer stem cell traits via targeting the Wnt/β-catenin signaling pathway and inducing G1 cell cycle arrest. Food Funct. 2018a;9(1):525–33." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d6478e623">13</a>]. Treatment of human gastric cancer cells SGC-7901 with cyanidin-3-glucoside markedly increased <i>KLF6</i> expression (an important tumor suppressor gene inactivated in many human cancers) and P21 protein levels, and inhibited cyclin-dependent kinase 4 and cyclin D1 expression [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang Y, Zhang XN, Xie WH, Zheng YX, Cao JP, Cao PR, et al. The growth of SGC-7901 tumor xenografts was suppressed by Chinese bayberry anthocyanin extract through upregulating KLF6 gene expression. Nutrients. 2016;8:599." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d6478e630">7</a>].</p><p>Flavonoids, including cyanidin-3-glucoside and anthocyanidins, may have beneficial effects on diabetes by reducing oxidative stress in muscle and fat [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Babu PV, Liu D, Gilbert ER. Recent advances in understanding the anti-diabetic actions of dietary flavonoids. J Nutr Biochem. 2013;24(11):1777–89." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d6478e636">14</a>]. Extracts from <i>M. rubra</i> showed protective effects on liver injuries [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Ohta S, Sakurai N, Kamogawa A, Yaguchi Y, Inoue T, Shinoda M. Protective effects of the bark of Myrica rubra Sieb. et Zucc. on experimental liver injuries. Yakugaku Zasshi. 1992;112(4):244–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d6478e642">15</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Xu L, Gao J, Wang Y, Yu W, Zhao X, Yang X, et al. Myrica rubra extracts protect the liver from CCl (4)-induced damage. Evid Based Complement Alternat Med. 2011;2011:518302." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d6478e645">16</a>] and non-alcoholic and alcohol-induced fatty liver disease [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Guo H, Zhong R, Liu Y, Jiang X, Tang X, Li Z, et al. Effects of bayberry juice on inflammatory and apoptotic markers in young adults with features of non-alcoholic fatty liver disease. Nutrition. 2014;30(2):198–203." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d6478e648">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Liu H, Qi X, Cao S, Li P. Protective effect of flavonoid extract from Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. et Zucc.) fruit on alcoholic liver oxidative injury in mice. J Nat Med. 2014;68(3):521–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d6478e652">18</a>], which may be due to its potent antioxidant properties. Thus, the major flavonoids, proanthocyanidins, and anthocyanins of Chinese bayberry leaves have strong chemical and cellular antioxidant activities [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Fu Y, Qiao L, Cao Y, Zhou X, Liu Y, Ye X. Structural elucidation and antioxidant activities of proanthocyanidins from Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. et Zucc.) leaves. PLoS One. 2014;9(5):e96162." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6478e655">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Zhang X, Lv Q, Jia S, Chen Y, Sun C, Li X, et al. Effects of flavonoid-rich Chinese bayberry (Morella rubra Sieb. et Zucc.) fruit extract on regulating glucose and lipid metabolism in diabetic KK-A(y) mice. Food Funct. 2016;7(7):3130–40." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d6478e658">19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Zhang B, Kang M, Xie Q, Xu B, Sun C, Chen K, et al. Anthocyanins from Chinese bayberry extract protect β cells from oxidative stress-mediated injury via HO-1 upregulation. J Agric Food Chem. 2011;59(2):537–45." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d6478e661">20</a>].</p><p>Flavonoids are abundant in Chinese bayberry fruits, but the genetic basis of their biosynthesis remains unclear. Two genes encoding key proanthocyanidins and biosynthetic enzymes, namely anthocyanidin reductase and leucoanthocyanidin reductase, have been isolated from Chinese bayberry fruit cDNA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Shi L, Cao S, Chen X, Chen W, Zheng Y, Yang Z. Proanthocyanidin synthesis in Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. et Zucc.) fruits. Front Plant Sci. 2018;9:212." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d6478e667">21</a>]. Partial cDNA sequences of the genes encoding the anthocyanin biosynthesis enzymes chalcone synthase, chalcone isomerase, flavanone 3-hydroxylase (F3H), flavonoid 3'-hydroxylase (F3'H), dihydroflavonol 4-reductase (DFR), anthocyanidin synthase (ANS), and uridine diphosphate glucose flavonoid 3-O-glucosyltransferase (UFGT), as well as <i>MrMYB1</i>, a R2R3 MYB transcription factor homologous to known anthocyanin biosynthesis activators, were isolated from ripe Chines bayberry ‘Biqizhong’ fruit. Differences in the mRNA abundance of <i>MrF3H</i>, <i>MrF3'H</i>, <i>MrDFR1</i>, <i>MrANS</i>, and <i>MrUFGT</i> as well as the transcript level of <i>MrMYB1</i>, were highly correlated with the differential accumulation of anthocyanins [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Niu SS, Xu CJ, Zhang WS, Zhang B, Li X, Lin-Wang K, et al. Coordinated regulation of anthocyanin biosynthesis in Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra) fruit by a R2R3 MYB transcription factor. Planta. 2010;231(4):887–99." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d6478e692">22</a>]. The sequences, active sites, and expression levels of the genes related to the antioxidant signaling pathway in Chinese bayberry remain unknown, but elucidating them would be useful for the development of new medication.</p><p>Recently, Jia et al. reported the genome sequence of a female ‘Shuijing’ Chinese bayberry individual bearing near-white fruit producing little anthocyanin [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Jia HM, Jia HJ, Cai QL, Wang Y, Zhao HB, Yang WF, et al. The red bayberry genome and genetic basis of sex determination. Plant Biotechnol J. 2018. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1111/pbi.12985&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d6478e699">23</a>]. The genome size was 313 Mb, 90 % of sequences were assembled into eight pseudo-chromosomes, 32,493 genes were predicted, and a 59-kb genomic region presumably related to sex determination in the female was identified [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Jia HM, Jia HJ, Cai QL, Wang Y, Zhao HB, Yang WF, et al. The red bayberry genome and genetic basis of sex determination. Plant Biotechnol J. 2018. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1111/pbi.12985&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d6478e702">23</a>]. In the present study we sequenced and assembled the genome of a different Chinese bayberry cultivar, ‘Zaojia’, bearing anthocyanins-rich fruit. We also conducted in-depth transcriptomic analyses to reveal the gene regulatory basis underlying its antioxidant and pharmacological activity.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>We adopted a whole-genome shotgun strategy to sequence and assemble the genome, yielding a total of 64,147 Mb at a depth (×) of 210.75 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S1). The assembled genome was 289.92 Mb, which was similar to the size of the assembled ‘Shuijing’ genome and covered 95.05 % of the estimated genome of ‘Zaojia’ (304.38 Mb) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S2; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S1a) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Jia HM, Jia HJ, Cai QL, Wang Y, Zhao HB, Yang WF, et al. The red bayberry genome and genetic basis of sex determination. Plant Biotechnol J. 2018. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1111/pbi.12985&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d6478e726">23</a>]. However, it was much smaller than the genomes of apple (603.9 Mb), grapevine (487 Mb), and orange (320.5 Mb), but larger than that of peach (227.4 Mb) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S3). We predicted 26,325 protein-encoding genes with an average gene length of 3,828 bp, exon length of 266 bp, and intron length of 488.17 bp (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S4; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Whole-genome duplication in Chinese bayberry</h3><p>Within the Chinese bayberry genome, 14,303 paralogous relationships were identified, covering 31 % of the genes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S5). Genomic synteny among <i>M. rubra</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (grapevine), and <i>Malus</i> × <i>domestica</i> (apple) revealed the genomic evolution of <i>M. rubra</i> (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig1">1</a>a and b). An ancestral genome region in the basal eudicot <i>V. vinifera</i> was aligned to a genome region of <i>M. rubra</i> indicating paleohexaploidization, which is common in eudicots, but no recent whole-genome duplication event was detected in <i>M. rubra</i> (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig1">1</a>a and b). One genome region of <i>M. rubra</i> aligned with two regions of <i>Malus × domestica</i>, indicating a recent whole-genome duplication event in the latter (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig1">1</a> a and 1b). A comparison of the Chinese bayberry genome sequence with that of <i>Morus notabilis</i> (mulberry) and <i>V. vinifera</i> revealed a large number of syntenic regions (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig1">1c</a>). The distribution of fourfold synonymous third-codon transversions (Figure 1c) and synonymous nucleotide substitution (Ks) rates (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S3) of homologous pairs in the syntenic regions, as well as the mean Ks values of individual syntenic regions of individual syntenic blocks, indicated that only the paleohexaploidization events common to eudicots occurred in the evolutionary history of Chinese bayberry. The whole-genome duplication event occurred before the <i>M. rubra</i>–<i>M. notabilis</i> or <i>M. rubra</i>–<i>V. vinifera</i> divergence and it is shared among eudicots.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="272" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Whole-genome duplication (WGD) in Chinese bayberry, <i>Myrica rubra</i>. (a) Macrosynteny of <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Myrica rubra</i>, and <i>Malus × domestica</i>. Genomic alignment of <i>V. vinifera</i>, <i>M. rubra</i>, and <i>M. × domestica</i> shows that one ancestral region in the basal eudicot <i>V. vinifera</i> can be aligned to one region in <i>M. rubra</i> derived from the paleohexaploidization common to eudicots. One region in <i>M. rubra</i> can be aligned to two regions in <i>M. × domestica</i>, indicating that WGD recently occurred in <i>M. × domestica</i>. (<b>b</b>) Microcollinearity analysis of <i>V. vinifera</i>, <i>M. rubra</i>, and <i>M. × domestica</i>. These regions represent one collinear set, highlighted in orange in (<b>a</b>). Navy blue rectangles indicate the predicted genes in the genome. One gene in <i>V. vinifera</i> corresponds to one gene in <i>M. rubra</i>, whereas one gene in <i>M. rubra</i> has two homologs in <i>M.</i> × <i>domestica</i>. (<b>c</b>) Density distribution of fourfold synonymous third-codon transversions (4DTV) for paralogous pairs among <i>M. rubra, M. notabilis</i>, and <i>V. vinifera</i>. The single peak of the <i>M. rubra–M. rubra</i> paralogs indicates no recent WGD in <i>M. rubra</i> except for the paleohexaploidization event, which is common to eudicots.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Comparative genomic analysis</h3><p>Gene similarity clustering of the 26,325 predicted genes for <i>M. rubra</i> with those of <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i>, and <i>M. notabilis</i> yielded 13,216 unique gene families (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S6). A five-way comparison showed that 4,074 gene families were shared by the five species, and 712 gene families were unique to <i>M. rubra</i>. This number was higher than in <i>F.</i> × <i>ananassa</i> (222), lower than in <i>S. lycopersicum</i> (1,198), and similar to that in <i>M. notabilis</i> (779) and <i>V. vinifera</i> (708) (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig2">2</a>a and b). Gene similarity clustering of the 26,325 predicted genes for <i>M. rubra</i> with those of <i>Cicer arietinum</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, and <i>Oryza sativa</i> yielded 12,266 unique gene families (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S7). A four-way comparison revealed that 4,270 gene families were shared by these four species, and 891 gene families were unique to <i>M. rubra</i>. This number was higher than that in <i>C. arietinum</i> (214) and lower than that in <i>A. thaliana</i> (933) and <i>O. sativa</i> (2,478) (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S4a and S4b).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="680" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Comparative genomic analysis of <i>Myrica rubra</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>Morus notabilis</i>, <i>Fragaria × ananassa</i>, and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>. (<b>a</b>) Clustering of gene families by OrthoMCL in <i>M. rubra</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>F. × ananassa</i>, and <i>V. vinifera</i>. (<b>b</b>) Statistics of the genes involved in clustering. (<b>c</b>) Annotation and classification of genes unique to <i>M. rubra</i> by KOG.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The classification of specific genes in the above two classes (the above five-way comparison and the four-way comparison) was similar in different gene families. The first four groups were involved in signal transduction mechanisms (800, 858), secondary metabolite biosynthesis, transport, and catabolism (500, 509), post-translational modification, protein turnover, and chaperones (461, 535), and carbohydrate transport and metabolism (445, 458) (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig2">2</a>c; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S4c). This indicates that these functions were enhanced during Chinese bayberry evolution. The antioxidant signaling pathways of flavonoids, anthocyanins, and terpenes in <i>M. rubra</i> comprise genes of the abovementioned four groups, and it is suggested that they are enhanced in <i>M. rubra</i> in comparison with other plant species.</p><p>A phylogenetic tree based on 560 single-copy protein sequences was constructed for nine species (<i>M. rubra</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>Prunus mume</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>Citrullus lanatus</i>, <i>F.</i> × <i>ananassa</i>, <i>Citrus sinensis</i>, <i>A. thaliana,</i> and <i>Carica papaya</i>) using PhyML. <i>Myrica rubra</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, and <i>M. notabilis</i> were clustered in a subclade that was most likely derived from a common ancestor approximately 93.95 Mya, whereas <i>P. mume</i> and <i>M. notabilis</i> diverged 82.41 Mya, and <i>C. lanatus</i> diverged from an ancestor shared with <i>M. rubra</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, and <i>M. notabilis</i> around 97.67 Mya. The two clades, one including <i>M. rubra</i>, <i>C. lanatus</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, and <i>M. notabilis</i> and the other including <i>C. sinensis</i>, <i>F.</i> × <i>ananassa</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, and <i>C. papaya</i>, were estimated to have diverged 103.30 Mya (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S5a).</p><p>A phylogenetic tree inferred from 1,737 single-copy protein sequences of six species (<i>M. rubra</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>C. lanatus</i>, and <i>F.</i> × <i>ananassa</i>) resolved <i>M. rubra</i> as sister to the clade of <i>P. mume</i> and <i>M. notabilis</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S5b). Comparison of the gene families in <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>C. lanatus</i>, and <i>F.</i> × <i>ananassa</i> with those in <i>M. rubra</i> led to the identification of 513 gene families that were expanded (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S8), such as leucine-rich repeats (six copies), toxic anion resistance protein, and reverse transcriptase, and 4,063 gene families that were contracted in <i>M. rubra</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S9). Moreover, the MYB/SANT-like DNA-binding domain, zinc-binding domain in reverse transcriptase, transposase family tnp2, and transposase-associated domain exhibited more significant gene family expansion when compared with those of other plants (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S8), indicating that these functions have been enhanced during Chinese bayberry evolution.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene analysis of antioxidant and pharmacological activity</h3><p>11The antioxidant activity of Chinese bayberry extract and its ability to inhibit cancer cell reproduction are linked to total flavonoids, anthocyanins, and terpenes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Ambrož M, Hanušová V, Skarka A, Boušová I, Králová V, Langhasová L, et al. Essential oil from Myrica rubra leaves potentiated antiproliferative and prooxidative effect of doxorubicin and its accumulation in intestinal cancer cells. Planta Med. 2016;82:89–96." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1254">24</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang X, Huang H, Zhang Q, Fan F, Xu C, Sun C, et al. Phytochemical characterization of Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. et Zucc.) of 17 cultivars and their antioxidant properties. Int J Mol Sci. 2015;16:12467–81." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1257">25</a>]. We found that the fruit of ‘Zaojia’ have relatively high total flavonoid contents, and that their antioxidant activity decreased during the course of development (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig3">3</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S10). According to the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) signaling pathway for flavonoid biosynthesis (reference map 00941), the copy number of the enzyme-coding genes within the flavonoid biosynthetic pathway in <i>M. rubra</i>, <i>C. lanatus</i>, <i>F.</i> × <i>ananassa</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> were analyzed and compared (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S11). In the flavonoid pathway of Chinese bayberry, shikimate <i>O</i>-hydroxycinnamoyltransferase (<i>HCT</i>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Hoffmann L, Besseau S, Geoffroy P, Ritzenthaler C, Meyer D, Lapierre C, et al. Silencing of hydroxycinnamoyl-coenzyme A shikimate/quinate hydroxycinnamoyltransferase affects phenylpropanoid biosynthesis. Plant Cell. 2004;16:1446–65." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1298">26</a>] was present in high copy numbers (19) (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig4">4a</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S11). Its main role is transferring acetyl groups from <i>p</i>-coumaroyl-CoA and caffeoyl CoA to shikimate and quinate [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang X, Huang H, Zhang Q, Fan F, Xu C, Sun C, et al. Phytochemical characterization of Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. et Zucc.) of 17 cultivars and their antioxidant properties. Int J Mol Sci. 2015;16:12467–81." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1311">25</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Bao J, Cai Y, Sun M, Wang G, Corke H. Anthocyanins, flavonols, and free radical scavenging activity of Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra) extracts and their color properties and stability. J Agric Food Chem. 2005;53:2327–32." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1314">27</a>]. In the phylogenetic tree based on the <i>HCT</i> gene families of the six plant species, most of the genes encoding HCT in <i>M. rubra</i> were clustered in one subclade (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig4">4</a>b), suggesting a tandem duplication event in this species. The gene structure and/or single amino acid change in functionally active residues of <i>HCT</i> (MRNA_014518) showed low similarity to genes of the other five plant species (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5</a>a). The expression levels of <i>HCT</i> (K13065) homologs during fruit development were generally low, and the expression levels of ten and nine HCT family members were negatively correlated with total flavonoids and total antioxidant capacity, respectively (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6</a>a; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S12). Thus, the high gene copy number and the specific active residues of each gene copy might not be correlated with high contents of flavonoids in Chinese bayberry fruit (Additional file 1: Table S10; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S6a and 6b).</p><div Values at the nodes indicate the percentage of bootstrap support from 1,000 replicates</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="827" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Gene structure and/or single amino acid changes in functionally active residues for gene expansions in flavonoid and anthocyanin biosynthesis pathways.
