(<b>c</b>) anthocyanidin 3-O-glucosyltransferase (BZ1, K12930). (<b>d</b>) Caffeoyl-CoA O-methyltransferase (CCoAOMT, K00588). (<b>e</b>) chalcone synthase (CHS, K00660). (<b>f</b>) anthocyanidin reductase (ANR, K08695). (<b>g</b>) chalcone isomerase (CHI, K01859). (<b>h</b>) bifunctional dihydroflavonol 4-reductase/flavanone 4-reductase (DFR, K13082). (<b>i</b>) leucoanthocyanidin dioxygenase (LDOX, K05277). (<b>j</b>) leucoanthocyanidin reductase (LAR, K13081). (<b>k</b>) flavonol synthase (FLS, K05278). (<b>l</b>) naringenin 3-dioxygenase (K00475). (<b>m</b>) trans-cinnamate 4-monooxygenase (CYP73A, K00487). (<b>n</b>) flavonoid 3',5'-hydroxylase (K13083). (<b>o</b>) flavonoid 3'-monooxygenase (K05280)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="564" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Expression profiles of functional genes involved in (<b>a</b>) flavonoid, (<b>b</b>) anthocyanin, (<b>c</b>) monoterpenoid, (<b>d</b>) diterpenoid, and (<b>e</b>) sesquiterpenoid and triterpenoid biosynthesis pathways in <i>Myrica rubra.</i> Numbers 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5 denote 10, 20, 30,40, and 50 days after pollination, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In the KEGG reference map 00942 for the anthocyanin synthesis signaling pathway, the copy number of anthocyanidin 3-<i>O</i>-glucoside 5-<i>O</i>-glucosyltransferase (<i>UGT75C1</i>; six copies) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Tohge T, Nishiyama Y, Hirai MY, Yano M, Nakajima J, Awazuhara M, et al. Functional genomics by integrated analysis of metabolome and transcriptome of Arabidopsis plants over-expressing an MYB transcription factor. Plant J. 2005;42:218–35." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1555">28</a>] and anthocyanidin 3-<i>O</i>-glucosyltransferase (<i>BZ1</i>; five copies) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Ford CM, Boss PK, Hoj PB. Cloning and characterization of Vitis vinifera UDP-glucose: flavonoid 3-O-glucosyltransferase, a homologue of the enzyme encoded by the maize Bronze-1 locus that may primarily serve to glucosylate anthocyanidins in vivo. J Bio Chem. 1998;273:9224–33." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1565">29</a>] was high (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig4">4</a>c; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S11). These two enzymes play important roles in anthocyanin synthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Sun C, Huang H, Xu C, Li X, Chen K. Biological activities of extracts from Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. et Zucc.): a review. Plant Food Hum Nutr. 2013;68:97–106." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1574">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Shi L, Cao S, Shao J, Chen W, Zheng Y, Jiang Y, et al. Relationship between sucrose metabolism and anthocyanin biosynthesis during ripening in Chinese bayberry fruit. J Agr Food Chem. 2014;62:10522–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1577">30</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Chen YY, Zhang ZH, Zhong CY, Song XM, Lin QH, Huang CM, et al. Functional analysis of differentially expressed proteins in Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb.et Zucc.) fruits during ripening. Food Chem. 2016;190:763–70." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1581">31</a>]. The phylogenetic tree based on UGT75C1 confirmed tandem duplication in some genes (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig4">4</a>d). Phylogenetic analysis of the UGT75C1 gene families resolved the homologous genes from <i>S. lycopersicum</i> that are closely related to the gene cluster of <i>M. rubra</i>. Variations in the functional residues of UGT75C1 (MRNA_017263) compared with its orthologs in <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, and <i>F.</i> × <i>ananassa</i> (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5</a>b), and <i>BZ1</i> (MRNA_008272_1) also showed low similarity across species (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5</a>c). Although UGT75C1 (K12338) had six copies, low expression levels were detected and the expression levels of four copies were negatively correlated with the decrease of total flavonoids and total antioxidant capacity during fruit development (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6</a>b; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13). Because BZ1 (K12930, MRNA_008272_1) is highly expressed throughout the ripening process, it may contribute to the accumulation of anthocyanin during fruit ripening (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6</a>b; Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig7">7</a>a; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13). The other four <i>BZ1</i> copies detected were positively correlated with the decrease of total flavonoids and total antioxidant capacity during fruit development (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S13). Thus, it is reasonable to argue that the high copy number and the variations on the functional residues of the <i>BZ1</i> gene (MRNA_008272_1) might have contributed to the high anthocyanin content in ‘Zaojia’ fruit (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S10; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S6a and 6b).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Fig. 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="347" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>Relative expression levels of eight genes. (<b>a</b>) MRNA-008272-1 (anthocyanidin 3-O-glucosyltransferase, BZ1, K12930). (<b>b</b>) MRNA-011576-1 (caffeoyl-CoA O-methyltransferase, K005888). (<b>c</b>) MRNA_004300_1 (chalcone synthase, K00660). (<b>d</b>) MRNA_014338_1 (flavonoid 3',5'-hydroxylase, K13083). (<b>e</b>) MRNA_026243_1 ((-)-germacrene D synthase, K15803). (<b>f</b>) MRNA-022650-1 ((+)-neomenthol dehydrogenase, K15095). (<b>g</b>) MRNA_015875_1 (gibberellin 2-oxidase, K04125). (<b>h</b>) MRNA_011718_2 (squalene monooxygenase, K00511)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The expression patterns of genes related to flavonoid biosynthesis indicated that the pathway is active in <i>M. rubra</i> during fruit ripening (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6b</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S12 and S13). Caffeoyl-CoA O-methyltransferase (CCoAOMT) (K005888, MRNA-011576-1), which is associated with the phenylpropanoid pathway and lignin production, showed high expression levels during fruit ripening (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6a</a>; Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig7">7b</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S12). Its functional residues are highly similar to those in the other five species (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5d</a>). Based on these results, we propose that the high expression levels of CCoAOMT may have contributed to the high flavonoid content in ‘Zaojia’ fruit (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S10; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S6a). In maize (<i>Zea mays</i>), <i>ZmCCoAOMT2</i> confers resistance to both southern leaf blight and gray leaf spot [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yang Q, He Y, Kabahuma M, Chaya T, Kelly A, Borrego E, et al. A gene encoding maize caffeoyl-CoA O-methyltransferase confers quantitative resistance to multiple pathogens. Nat Genet. 2017;49(9):1364–72." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1734">32</a>]. In <i>V. vinifera</i>, VvCCoAOMT is a multifunctional O-methyltransferase that may contribute to anthocyanin methylation activity in grape berries, particularly under drought stress conditions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yang Q, He Y, Kabahuma M, Chaya T, Kelly A, Borrego E, et al. A gene encoding maize caffeoyl-CoA O-methyltransferase confers quantitative resistance to multiple pathogens. Nat Genet. 2017;49(9):1364–72." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1740">32</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Giordano D, Provenzano S, Ferrandino A, Vitali M, Pagliarani C, Roman F, et al. Characterization of a multifunctional caffeoyl-CoA O-methyltransferase activated in grape berries upon drought stress. Plant Physiol Biochem. 2016;101:23–32." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1743">33</a>]. Resistance in ‘Zaojia’ fruit might be due to variations in <i>CCoAOMT</i> (MRNA-011576-1) gene expression levels, which result in differences in the levels of lignin and other metabolites of the phenylpropanoid pathway and in the regulation of programmed cell death. Chalcone synthase is the key enzyme of flavonoid/isoflavonoid biosynthetic pathways, and it functions in the initial step of flavonoid biosynthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Dao TT, Linthorst HJ, Verpoorte R. Chalcone synthase and its functions in plant resistance. Phytochem Rev. 2011;10(3):397–412." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1750">34</a>]. Chalcone synthase functional residues of <i>M. rubra</i> have high similarity to those in the other five species (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5e</a>). In <i>M. rubra</i>, chalcone synthase (K00660, MRNA_004300_1) was highly expressed during the early stage of fruit development (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6</a>a; Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig7">7</a>c), suggesting that this gene is important for the high total flavonoid content and total antioxidant capacity of young fruit (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S10; Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig3">3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig3">3b</a>). The attenuated expression of the gene in later fruit development stages was correlated with the decrease of total flavonoids (r = 0.799) and total antioxidant capacity (r = 0.659) (Additional file 1: Table S12). Enzymes K05278 (flavonol synthase, MRNA_021063_1), K00475 (naringenin 3-dioxygenase, MRNA_013165_1), K01859 (chalcone isomerase, MRNA_020916_2), K05277 (leucoanthocyanidin dioxygenase, MRNA_009502_1), K05280 (flavonoid 3'-monooxygenase, MRNA_009278_3), K13081 (leucoanthocyanidin reductase, MRNA_012654_1), K13082 (bifunctional DFR/flavanone 4-reductase, MRNA_001369_2 and MRNA_001461_1), K00487 (trans-cinnamate 4-monooxygenase, MRNA_010278_2, gene <i>CYP73A</i>), K13083 (flavonoid 3',5'-hydroxylase, MRNA_014338_1), and K08695 (anthocyanidin reductase, MRNA_017633_1) are crucial for flavonoid biosynthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Pascual MB, El-Azaz J, de la Torre FN, Cañas RA, Avila C, Cánovas FM. Biosynthesis and metabolic fate of phenylalanine in conifers. Front Plant Sci. 2016;7:1030." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1781">35</a>]. They all exhibited high expression levels in early fruit development, and their decreased expressions were correlated with the decrease of total flavonoids and total antioxidant capacity during fruit maturation (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig3">3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig3">3b</a>; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S12). These genes are suggested to be important for the high early total flavonoid content in fruit development. The functional residues of anthocyanidin reductase (ANR, K08695) (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5f</a>), chalcone isomerase (CHI, K01859) (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5g</a>), bifunctional dihydroflavonol 4-reductase/flavanone 4-reductase (DFR, K13082) (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5</a>h), leucoanthocyanidin dioxygenase (LDOX, K05277) (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5</a>i), and leucoanthocyanidin reductase (LAR, K13081) (Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5</a>j) were relatively similar to that of homologous genes in the other five species. The functional residues of flavonol synthase (K0578, Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5</a>k), naringenin 3-dioxygenase (K00475, Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5l</a>), CYP73A (K00487, Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5</a>m), flavonoid 3’5’-hydroxylase (K13083, Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5n</a>), and flavonoid 3’-monooxygenase (K05280, Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig5">5o</a>) showed low similarity to homologous genes in the other five species. The leucoanthocyanidin dioxygenase of <i>Reaumuria trigyna</i> (Enzyme Commission number 1.14.11.19) is a multifunctional dioxygenase in flavonoid biosynthesis that is involved in enhancing plant responses to NaCl stress [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Zhang X, Lv Q, Jia S, Chen Y, Sun C, Li X, et al. Effects of flavonoid-rich Chinese bayberry (Morella rubra Sieb. et Zucc.) fruit extract on regulating glucose and lipid metabolism in diabetic KK-A(y) mice. Food Funct. 2016;7(7):3130–40." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1829">19</a>].</p><p>Different alleles encoding leucoanthocyanidin reductase influence resistance against the fungus <i>Heterobasidion parviporum</i> in <i>Picea abies</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Nemesio-Gorriz M, Hammerbacher A, Ihrmark K, Källman T, Olson Å, Lascoux M, et al. Different alleles of a gene encoding leucoanthocyanidin reductase (PaLAR3) influence resistance against the fungus Heterobasidion parviporum in Picea abies. Plant Physiol. 2016;171(4):2671–81." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1841">36</a>]. The expression profiling of flavonoid hydroxylase genes is influenced by diverse abiotic stresses, including cold, salinity, drought, UV-B radiation, and plant hormone abscisic acid or jasmonic acid treatments [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Liu H, Qi X, Cao S, Li P. Protective effect of flavonoid extract from Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb. et Zucc.) fruit on alcoholic liver oxidative injury in mice. J Nat Med. 2014;68(3):521–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1844">18</a>]. The overexpression of <i>RrANR</i> in <i>Rosa rugosa</i> results in increased plant tolerance to oxidative stress via increased scavenging of reactive oxygen species and modulation of the abscisic acid signaling pathway [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Luo P, Shen Y, Jin S, Huang S, Cheng X, Wang Z, et al. Overexpression of Rosa rugosa anthocyanidin reductase enhances tobacco tolerance to abiotic stress through increased ROS scavenging and modulation of ABA signaling. Plant Sci. 2016;245:35–49." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1854">37</a>].</p><p>We suggest that K005888 (MRNA-011576-1), which encodes CCoAOMT is the most important enzyme related to flavonoid biosynthesis, while K12930 (BZ1, MRNA_008272_1) is the key enzyme related to anthocyanin biosynthesis during fruit development. Stress resistance in Chinese bayberry fruits might be due to variations in the expression levels of these genes and corresponding amino acid sequences, resulting in differences in the levels of flavonoids and other metabolites of the phenylpropanoid pathway and in the regulation of programmed cell death.</p><p>Regarding the terpenoid signaling pathway, the copy numbers of (3<i>S</i>)-linalool synthase (5) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Chen YY, Zhang ZH, Zhong CY, Song XM, Lin QH, Huang CM, et al. Functional analysis of differentially expressed proteins in Chinese bayberry (Myrica rubra Sieb.et Zucc.) fruits during ripening. Food Chem. 2016;190:763–70." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1867">31</a>] of the monoterpene pathway (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig8">8</a>a; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S11), cytochrome P450, 82 family, subfamily g, polypeptide 1 (CYP82G1) (6) of the diterpenes signaling pathway [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Lee S, Badieyan S, Bevan DR, Herde M, Gatz C, Tholl D, et al. Herbivore-induced and floral homoterpene volatiles are biosynthesized by a single P450 enzyme (CYP82G1) in Arabidopsis. Pro Natl Acad Sci USA. 2010;107:21205–10." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1876">38</a>] (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig8">8</a>b; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S11), and (-)-germacrene D synthase (K15803) (25) of the sesquiterpene and triterpene synthesis pathway [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lücker J, Bowen P, Bohlmann J. Vitis vinifera terpenoid cyclases: functional identification of two sesquiterpene synthase cDNAs encoding (+)-valencene synthase and (+)-germacrene D synthase and expression of mono- and sesquiterpene synthases in grapevine flowers and berries. Phytochemistry. 2004;65:2649–59." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d6478e1886">39</a>] (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig8">8</a>c; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S11) were very high in Chinese bayberry. The phylogenetic trees based on (3<i>S</i>)-linalool synthase (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig8">8</a>d), CYP82G1 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig8">8</a>e), and (-)-germacrene D synthase (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig8">8</a>f) showed a tandem duplication in <i>M. rubra</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Fig. 8"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 8</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig8_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig8_HTML.png" alt="figure8" loading="lazy" width="685" height="511" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p>Gene expansions in monoterpenoid, diterpenoid, and sesquiterpenoid/triterpenoid biosynthesis pathways. (<b>a</b>) Monoterpenoid biosynthesis pathways in plants. Enzyme Commission number (EC) 4.2.3.25, (3<i>S</i>)-linalool synthase. <b>(b)</b> Diterpenoid biosynthesis in plants. EC 1.14.11.15, gibberellin 3-beta-dioxygenase; EC 1.14.11.13, gibberellin 2-oxidase; CYP82G1, cytochrome P450, family 82, subfamily G, polypeptide 1; EC 4.2.3.144, geranyllinalool synthase. (<b>c</b>) Sesquiterpenoid/triterpenoid biosynthesis. EC 4.2.3.22, 4.2.3.75, (-)-germacrene D synthase; EC 1.1.1.216, farnesol dehydrogenase. Phylogenetic analysis of (3<i>S</i>)-linalool synthase family <b>(d)</b>, CYP82G1 (<b>e</b>), and (-)-germacrene D synthase family (<b>f</b>) in <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>Prunus mume</i>, <i>Morus notabilis</i>, <i>Myrica rubra</i>, <i>Citrullus lanatus</i>, and <i>Fragaria</i> × <i>ananassa</i>. Values at the nodes indicate the percentage of bootstrap support from 1,000 replicates</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The functional residues of (3<i>S</i>)-linalool synthase (MRNA_017575_1) in <i>M. rubra</i> contained some variations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig9">9</a>a), and those of CYP82G1 (MRNA_015141_1) showed low similarity to orthologs in the other five species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig9">9</a>b). However, the expression level of (3<i>S</i>)-linalool synthase (K15086) was very low throughout the process of fruit development (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6</a>c, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S14), while CYP82G1 (K17961, MRNA_015141_1) was expressed at an intermediate level. The latter was weakly correlated with the total antioxidant capacity during fruit development (r = 0.408) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6d</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S15). The phylogenetic tree based on (-)-germacrene D synthase (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig8">8</a>f) revealed that both the Rosales clade (<i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, and <i>F. × ananassa</i>) and the Fagales clade (<i>M. rubra</i>) expanded during the course of evolution. This genome expansion may not be entirely clade-specific as the sub-branches of gene trees were expanded in only one species. The (-)-germacrene D synthase (MRNA_026243_1, K15803) gene showed high expression during the first 20 days after pollination and its expression level decreased subsequently. The correlation coefficient between gene expression and total antioxidant capacity was 0.853 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6</a>e; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig7">7</a>e; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S16), indicating that the enzyme is important in the early sesquiterpenoid/triterpenoid biosynthesis and in the formation of the total antioxidant capacity in early stage fruit (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig3">3</a>b).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-9" data-title="Fig. 9"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig9" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 9</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/9" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig9_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-9-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-019-5818-7/MediaObjects/12864_2019_5818_Fig9_HTML.png" alt="figure9" loading="lazy" width="685" height="265" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-9-desc"><p>Gene structure and/or single amino acid change in functionally active residues for genes in monoterpenoid, diterpenoid, sesquiterpenoid, and triterpenoid biosynthesis pathways. (<b>a</b>) (<i>3S</i>)-linalool synthase (TPS14, K15086), (<b>b</b>) cytochrome P450, family 82, subfamily G, polypeptide 1 (K17961), (<b>c</b>) (+)-neomenthol dehydrogenase (K15095), (<b>d</b>) gibberellin 2-oxidase (K04125), and (<b>e</b>) squalene monooxygenase (SQLE, ERG1, K00511)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/figures/9" data-track-dest="link:Figure9 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The genes implicated in monoterpenoid (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6</a>c), diterpenoid (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6</a>d), sesquiterpenoid, and triterpenoid (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6</a>e) biosynthesis pathways were also active in <i>M. rubra</i>. For example, K15095 [(+)-neomenthol dehydrogenase, MRNA_022650_1] (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6</a>c ; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig7">7</a>f; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S14), K04125 (gibberellin 2-oxidase, MRNA_015875_1) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6</a>d ; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig7">7</a>g; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S15), and K00511 (squalene monooxygenase, MRNA_011718_2) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig6">6</a>e; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig7">7</a>h; Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S16) were highly expressed throughout fruit development and respectively participate in monoterpenoid biosynthesis, diterpenoid biosynthesis class metabolism, and sesquiterpenoid and triterpenoid biosynthesis pathways, and they may be important for the total antioxidant capacity during fruit development (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig3">3</a>b). The functional residues of the (+)-neomenthol dehydrogenase (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig9">9</a>c), gibberellin 2-oxidase (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig9">9</a>d), and squalene monooxygenase (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Fig9">9</a>e) genes showed low similarity to that in the other five plant species.</p><p>Overall, gene analysis indicated that, to obtain Chinese bayberry fruit with high levels of flavonoids, monoterpenoids, diterpenoids, sesquiterpenoids, and triterpenoids and to enhance fruit resistance, CCoAOMT, BZ1, (+)-neomenthol dehydrogenase, gibberellin 2-oxidase, and squalene monooxygenase should be considered in molecular breeding programs.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>This paper reports a compressive genome sequencing study of Chinese bayberry and the transcriptomic analysis of antioxidant pathways in Chinese bayberry fruit. The results obtained provide basis for functional genomic research and genetic improvement of <i>M. rubra</i>, and are valuable for inferring evolutionary relationships across related species. The genome sequence provided here, together with the existing genome assembly in the database, are crucial for mining DNA sequence variations between varieties and particular sequences for developing molecular markers to screen Chinese bayberry varieties rich in total flavonoids. Whereas the published genome sequencing project for Chinese bayberry focused on sex determination, this study paid primary attention to the analysis of antioxidant pathways for improved understanding of the resistance mechanisms. Most <i>M. rubra</i> genes in the antioxidant signaling pathways have multiple copies via tandem duplication events, and many genes contain structural and amino acid changes of functionally active residues with low similarity to the genes of <i>F. × ananassa</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, and <i>C. lanatus</i>. The expression levels of most genes were high during early fruit development and consistent with the high levels of total flavonoids and antioxidant capacity. The genes coding for CCoAOMT, BZ1, (+)-neomenthol dehydrogenase, gibberellin 2-oxidase, and squalene monooxygenase, are suggested as the major regulators of flavonoid, anthocyanin, monoterpenoid, diterpenoid, and sesquiterpenoid/triterpenoid contents, respectively, throughout fruit development. The revealed genome sequences of the representative antioxidant signaling pathways hold great potential for the discovery of useful traits and functional genes in Chinese bayberry.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Plant materials for genome and transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>Fresh leaves of 10-year-old <i>M. rubra</i> ‘Zaojia’ individuals were collected from Lanxi County, Zhejiang Province, China, for genome sequencing. The fruit were not visible to the naked eye until the 10th day after pollination, and therefore all samples were taken on the 10th day and set as reference samples. For transcriptome sequencing, fruit samples were collected 10, 20, 30, 40, and 50 days after the initial pollination event on April 3, 2016. Each sample comprised three biological replicates, which were collected from different trees and consisted of fruit from eastern, western, southern, and northern sides of the tree. Samples were denucleated, ground, immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen, and stored in an ultra-low temperature freezer (Haier 86L-386; Haier, Qingdao, China). These samples were also used for measurements of flavonoid content and total antioxidant capacity.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">DNA extraction and Illumina short paired-end (PE) reads sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted using the Cetyl trimethylammonium bromide DNA extraction protocol, purified, and quantified using Qubit 2.0 (Invitrogen, Carlsbad, CA, USA). Eleven standard libraries (with insert sizes of 200 bp, 220 bp, 500 bp, 3 kb, 4 kb, 8 kb, 10 kb, 15 kb, and 17 kb) were constructed using Illumina’s paired-end and mate-pair kits (Illumina, San Diego, CA, USA). These libraries were PE sequenced (2 × 150 bp) on the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform, yielding 64.18 Gb of raw data with 210.75-fold coverage of the <i>M. rubra</i> genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome assembly and evaluation</h3><p>A <i>de novo</i> assembly pipeline was developed for genome assembly. A whole-genome shotgun assembler ALLPATHS-LG (allpathslg-52488) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Gnerre S, Maccallum I, Przybylski D, Ribeiro F, Burton J, Walker B, et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Pro Natl Acad Sci USA. 2011;108:1513–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2200">40</a>] was used to generate contigs. These contigs were used for scaffolding in SSPACE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Boetzer M, Henkel C, Jansen H, Butler D, Pirovano W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics. 2011;27:578–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2203">41</a>] with reads of long mate-pair libraries. GapCloser 1.12 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Luo R, Liu B, Xie Y, Li Z, Huang W, Yuan J, et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience. 2012;1:18." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2206">42</a>] was used for gap filling. The 14,097 transcripts &gt; 1 kb were assembled using Trinity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Grabherr M, Haas B, Yassour M, Levin J, Thompson D, Amit I, et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol. 2011;29:644–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2209">43</a>] and aligned to the assembly with BLAT [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Kent W. BLAT -The BLAST-Like Alignment Tool. Genome Res. 2002;12:656–64." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2213">44</a>]. These procedures, and CEGMA 2.3 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Parra G, Bradnam K, Korf I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics. 2007;23:1061–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2216">45</a>], were used for genome assembly evaluation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Comparative genomics</h3><p>OrthoMCL 2.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Li L, Stoeckert CJ Jr, Roos DS. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 2003;13:2178–89." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2227">46</a>] was used to cluster gene families. All-against-all comparisons of proteins were performed using the basic local alignment search tool (BLAST) with a p-cutoff value of 1e-5. Results were filtered using thresholds with an E-value &lt; 1e-5 and percent match length ≥ 50 % (i.e., 50 % of all possible pairs within the group that were matched through BLAST), and potential in-paralog, ortholog, and co-ortholog pairs were found with the OrthoMCL 2.0 Pairs program. These pairs were grouped based on their relationships using the Markov Cluster Algorithm (MCL, <a href="https://micans.org/mcl/">https://micans.org/mcl/</a>) program. The single-copy gene families were identified based on the OrthoMCL clustering results. MUSCLE 3.8 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Edgar RC. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004;32:1792–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2237">47</a>] was used to perform the multiple alignments of each single copy gene group. Phylogenetic trees were calculated using the maximum likelihood algorithm of PhyML 4.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Guindon S, Gascuel O. A simple, fast, and accurate algorithm to estimate large phylogenies by maximum likelihood. Syst Biol. 2003;52:696–704." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2240">48</a>]. Divergence time was estimated using CODEML and MCMCTREE, as implemented in the PAML package [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Yang Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol Biol Evol. 2007;24:1586–91." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2243">49</a>], and substitution rates between gene pairs were calculated with CODEML. The substitution rate of each branch was set in CODEML based on protein sequences; because the global clock model was chosen, substitution rates were the same for all branches. Ambiguous characters and alignment gaps were removed, and the calibration time was obtained from the TimeTree database (<a href="http://www.timetree.org">http://www.timetree.org</a>). The evolution of gene family size in <i>M. rubra</i> and related species was evaluated using CAFE [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="De Bie T, Cristianini N, Demuth JP, Hahn MW. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics. 2006;22:1269–71." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2257">50</a>] based on the maximum likelihood phylogenetic trees with estimated branch lengths. The ancestral gene family sizes were inferred by CAFE using the probabilistic graphic model (p-value). Smaller p-values in gene families indicated more dramatic changes. The Viterbi algorithm was adopted to identify expanded and contracted gene families.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Flavonoid content and total antioxidant capacity of <i>M. rubra</i> fruits</h3><p>Each Chinese bayberry fruit sample was dried to a constant weight and ground into fine powder. Flavonoids were extracted from fruit samples using the Plant Flavonoid Kit (LHT-2-G; Suzhou Keming Biological Technology Co. Ltd, Suzhou, China) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. The standard curve for total flavonoid content was: y = 5.02x + 0.0007, R<sup>2</sup> = 0.9996. The flavonoid content (mg/g dry weight) = (∆A – 0.0007) ÷5.02 ×<i>V sample</i> ÷ (<i>V sample</i> ÷ <i>V sample total</i> × <i>W</i>), where ∆A: absorbance at 510 nm of sample against sterilized distilled water, <i>V</i> sample total is the volume of extraction liquid (2 mL), <i>V sample</i> is the sample volume in the reaction (0.54 mL), and <i>W</i> is sample quantity (g).</p><p>The total antioxidant capacity of each fruit sample was determined using the Total Antioxidant Capacity Kit (TAOC-2-G; Suzhou Keming Biological Technology Co. Ltd) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. The standard curve for the total antioxidant capacity was: y = 0.638x + 0.0645, R<sup>2</sup> = 0.9989. Total antioxidant capacity (U/g fresh weight) was obtained as =<span class="mathjax-tex">\( \frac{1}{0.6308} \)</span>(∆A – 0.0645) ×<i>V total anti</i> ÷ (<i>V sample</i> ÷ <i>V sample total</i> × <i>W</i>), where Δ<i>A is absorbance at 593 nm of sample against sterilized distilled water, V</i> sample total is the volume of extraction liquid (1 mL), <i>V total</i> anti is the total volume of the reaction (1.02 mL), <i>V sample</i> is the sample volume in the reaction (0.03 mL), and <i>W</i> is sample quantity (g).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">RNA extraction, transcriptome sequencing, assembly, and annotation</h3><p>The total RNA of <i>M. rubra</i> fruit was separately isolated from 15 samples using Trizol reagent (Invitrogen) according to the instructions of the manufacturer. The integrity and concentration of RNA samples were checked in the Agilent 2100 Bioanalyzer (Agilent Technologies, Inc., Santa Clara, CA, USA). Fifteen RNA-sequencing RNA-seq libraries were constructed and then sequenced with in the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform (Illumina). Raw reads were processed to trim low-quality bases at the ends of reads and adaptors via an in-house script. Low quality reads with Ns accounting for 10 % or more, reads where at least half of the bases scored &lt; 10 in PHRED, and reads with adaptor contamination were discarded. The clean RNA-seq data were then mapped to the genome assembly using Tophat2 2.0.14 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kim D, Pertea G, Trapnell C, Pimentel H, Kelley R, Salzberg SL. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 2013;14:R36." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2364">51</a>] to perform reference-guided mapping; the TopHat alignment was assembled using Cufflinks 2.2.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Trapnell C, Roberts A, Goff L, Pertea G, Kim D, Kelley DR, et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat Protoc. 2012;7:562–78." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2367">52</a>]. The sequencing and mapping statistics are summarized in Table S17.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Expression profiles of functional genes</h3><p>Cuffquan and CuffnormGene modules of Cufflinks 2.2.1 were used to quantify the expression levels of individual genes, and fragments per kilobase of transcript per million fragments mapped (FPKM) values served as the measurement. The values were log<sub>2</sub>FPKM transformed, and genes with FPKM &lt; 1 in at least five samples were removed. Expression profiles of functional genes involved in flavonoid, anthocyanin, monoterpenoid, diterpenoid, and sesquiterpenoid/triterpenoid biosynthesis pathways in <i>M. rubra</i> are displayed in tables and heat maps. The correlations between functional gene expressions and total flavonoid content or total antioxidant capacity were analyzed using SPSS Statistics 17.0 software. The correlation coefficients were between -1 and +1.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Homologs of genes and protein sequences</h3><p>Homologs of genes involved in flavonoid and terpenoid metabolic pathways were detected in <i>M. rubra</i>, <i>M. notabilis</i>, <i>P. mume</i>, <i>F. × ananassa, C. lanatus</i>, and <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, and multiple sequence alignments of homologs were performed using ClustalW2 (<a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/Tools/msa/clustalw2/">https://www.ebi.ac.uk/Tools/msa/clustalw2/</a>). Phylogenetic analysis of of genes involved in flavonoid and terpenoid metabolic pathways were performed using the neighbor-joining method of MEGA software (Molecular Evolutinary Genetics Analysis, version 6.0). Protein sequences of the genes putatively involved in <i>M. rubra</i> pathways were submitted to SWISS-MODEL (<a href="https://swissmodel.expasy.org/">https://swissmodel.expasy.org/</a>), and protein structure homology modeling was performed. The protein database consisting of experimentally checked protein structures was searched to select templates (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#MOESM1">1</a>: Table S18). Suitable templates were sorted by predicted global quality estimation scores.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Quantitative RT-PCR</h3><p>Complementary DNA sequences were obtained from the total RNA samples using the FastKing RT Kit with gDNase (KR180123) according to the manufacturer’s instructions (TIANGEN Biotech Co., Ltd., Beijing, China). A quantitative PCR was performed with SuperReal PreMix Plus (including SYBR Green) according to the manufacturer’s instructions (FP171206; TIANGEN Biotech Co., Ltd.) on a Roche LightCycler 96 (Roche Molecular Systems, Inc., Pleasanton, CA, USA). The 20-μL total volume reaction comprised 2 × SuperReal PreMix Plus (10 μL), primer solutions (0.6 μL for each primer), cDNA (2 μL), and RNase-free double-distilled water (6.8 μL). The PCR profile included pre-degeneration (95 °C for 30 s, 1 cycle), three-step amplification (95 °C for 5 s, 60 °C for 30 s, and 72 °C for 30 s, 40 cycles), melting (95 °C for 1 s, 65 °C for 15 s, 95 °C for 1 s), and cooling (37 °C for 30 s). The internal reference gene was MRNA_021433_1 (MrUBQ1), and the eight target genes chosen to validate the transcriptome data were MRNA-008272-1 (anthocyanidin 3-O-glucosyltransferase, BZ1, K12930), MRNA-011576-1 (caffeoyl-CoA O-methyltransferase, K005888), MRNA_004300_1 (chalcone synthase, K00660), MRNA_014338_1 (flavonoid 3',5'-hydroxylase, K13083), MRNA_026243_1 ((-)-germacrene D synthase, K15803), MRNA-022650-1 ((+)-neomenthol dehydrogenase, K15095), MRNA_015875_1 (gibberellin 2-oxidase, K04125), and MRNA_011718_2 (squalene monooxygenase, K00511). The relative expression. of the target genes was evaluated using the 2<sup>-ΔΔCt</sup> method [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Livak KJ, Schmittgen TDS. Analysis of relative gene expression data using real-time quantitative PCR and the analysis of relative gene expression data using real-time quantitative PCR and the 2−ΔΔCT method, vol. 25; 2001. p. 402–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d6478e2437">53</a>], where ΔΔCt = ΔCtT - ΔCtR, and ΔCtT = Ct (test sample, target gene) - Ct (test sample, reference gene), ΔCtR = Ct (reference sample, target gene) - Ct (reference sample, reference gene). The primers used and the product sizes are displayed in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7#Tab1">1</a>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Primers used and the product sizes of the eight target genes examined in the quantitative PCR.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-019-5818-7/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section>
                    <section aria-labelledby="availability-of-data-and-materials" data-title="Availability of data and materials"><div class="c-article-section" id="availability-of-data-and-materials-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="availability-of-data-and-materials">Availability of data and materials</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="availability-of-data-and-materials-content">
              
              <p>The datasets generated and analyzed during the current study are available in the NCBI database under BioProject PRJNA362655 (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/</a>).</p>

HY, PY, and HZ contributed to sequencing and information analyses. SL and XZ contributed to phenotyping. SJZ, and SWZ performed the quantitative PCR for the Chinese bayberry samples and the tests of total flavonoids and total antioxidant capacity. HR, XQ, and HY wrote the manuscript. All the authors have read and approved the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar2">Ethics approval and consent to participate</h3>
                <p>This study was carried out in full compliance with the laws of the People’s Republic of China. No specific permits were required for our field investigation. The study species is not included in the ‘List of Protected Plants in China’.</p>
              
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar3">Consent for publication</h3>
                <p>Not applicable.</p>
              
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar4">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>The authors declare that they have no competing interests.</p>
